Original File: datasets/MATH/MATH/test/intermediate_algebra/1034.json
Extracting Answer from: 
The focus of the parabola $y^2 = 4ax$ is $F = (a,0),$ and the directrix is $x = -a.$  Let $F',$ $P',$ $Q',$ and $R'$ be the projections of $F,$ $P,$ $Q,$ and $R$ onto the directrix, respectively.  Let $p = PP' = PF,$ $q = QQ' = QF,$ $a = P'F',$ and $B = Q'F'.$  Since $P,$ $F,$ and $Q$ are collinear,
\[\frac{p}{q} = \frac{a}{b}.\][asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

real y;
pair F, P, Q, R, S;
pair Fp, Pp, Qp, Rp;

F = (1,0);

path parab = ((-4)^2/4,-4);

for (y = -4; y <= 4; y = y + 0.01) {
  parab = parab--(y^2/4,y);
}

P = intersectionpoint(F--(F + 5*(1,2)),parab);
Q = intersectionpoint(F--(F - 5*(1,2)),parab);
R = reflect((0,0),(1,0))*(P);
S = extension(Q,R,(0,0),(1,0));
Fp = (-1,0);
Pp = (-1,P.y);
Qp = (-1,Q.y);
Rp = (-1,R.y);

draw(parab,red);
draw(P--Q);
draw(P--R);
draw(S--R);
draw((-2,0)--(4,0));
draw((0,-4)--(0,4));
draw((-1,-4)--(-1,4),dashed);
draw(P--Pp);
draw(Q--Qp);
draw(R--Rp);

label("$x = -a$", (-1,-4), dir(270));
label("$p$", (P + Pp)/2, N, red);
label("$p$", (P + F)/2, SE, red);
label("$q$", (Q + Qp)/2, dir(270), red);
label("$q$", (Q + F)/2, SE, red);
label("$a$", (Pp + Fp)/2, W, red);
label("$b$", (Qp + Fp)/2, W, red);
label("$p$", (Rp + R)/2, dir(270), red);

dot("$F$", F, SE);
dot("$P$", P, N);
dot("$Q$", Q, dir(270));
dot("$R$", R, dir(270));
dot("$F'$", S, NW);
dot("$P'$", Pp, W);
dot("$Q'$", Qp, W);
dot("$R'$", Rp, W);
[/asy]

Then
\[\frac{F'Q'}{F'R'} = \frac{b}{a} = \frac{q}{p} = \frac{QQ'}{RR'}.\]This means triangles $F'Q'Q$ and $F'R'R$ are similar, so line $QR$ intersects the $x$-axis at $F' = \boxed{(-a,0)}.$

GPT File: datasets/persona_short_answer/persona_short_answer/intermediate_algebra/1034_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Reflect $Q$ in the $x$-axis to get $Q'.$

[asy]
unitsize(0.6 cm);

real parab (real x) {
  return(x^2/4);
}

pair F, P, Q, Qp, R;

F = (0,1/4);
P = (1,1);
Q = (-1/2,1);
Qp = (-1/2,-1);
R = (1,-1);

draw(reflect((0,0),(1,0))*graph(parab,-2*sqrt(2),2*sqrt(2)));
draw((F + reflect((0,0),(1,0))*Qp)--(F + reflect((0,0),(1,0))*Q));
draw(Q--R--Qp);
draw(rightanglemark(Q,R,Qp,4));

dot("$F$", F, N);
dot("$P$", P, dir(0));
dot("$Q$", Q, NW);
dot("$Q'$", Qp, SW);
dot("$R$", R, dir(0));
[/asy]

Let $y = mx + k$ be the equation of the line passing through the focus of the parabola, and suppose $x = t$ is the $x$-coordinate of $Q.$  Then
\[t = \frac{y^2}{4a}.\]So $(t,y)$ satisfies
\[y - mx - k = 0.\]Substituting $t = \frac{y^2}{4a}$ and simplifying, we get the quadratic equation
\[4am^2 y^2 - 16akm y + 16ak^2 - y^4 = 0.\]By Vieta's formulas, $P$ and $Q$ are the two roots of this quadratic equation, so
\begin{align*}
\frac{y_P + y_Q}{m} &= \frac{4akm}{4am^2} = \frac{k}{m}, \\
y_P y_Q &= \frac{(16ak)^2}{(4am^2)(4a)} = \frac{16k^2}{m^2}.
\end{align*}Thus, by Vieta's formulas, $y_R$ is the other root of
\[4am^2 y^2 - 16akm y + 16ak^2 - y^4 = 0.\]That is,
\[y_R^4 - 4aky_R^2 + 4akm y_R - 4ak^2 = 0.\]Since $R$ is the reflection of $P$ in the $x$-axis, $y_P = -y_R.$  Also, $\frac{y_P}{y_Q} = 4,$ so
\[y_Q = \frac{4}{5} y_R.\]Then $y_P y_Q = -\frac{4}{5} y_R^2,$ so we want to solve
\begin{align*}
\frac{16}{5} y_R^2 &= \frac{(16ak)^2}{(4am^2)(4a)} = \frac{16k^2}{m^2}, \\
y_R^4 - 4aky_R^2 + 4akm y_R - 4ak^2 &= 0.
\end{align*}The first equation simplifies to $m^2 = 5akt.$  Substituting into the second equation, we get
\[y_R^4 - 20akt y_R^2 + 20atk^2 y_R - 20ak^3 = 0.\]Dividing by $y_R^2,$ we get the quadratic equation $y_R^2 - 20akt + 20ak^2 = 0,$ so by the quadratic formula,
\[y_R^2 = (10a)^2 - (2 \cdot 10a) t = 100a^2 - 20at.\]Then
\[y_Q = \frac{4}{5} y_R = \frac{4}{5} (10a - t) = 8a - \frac{4}{5} t,\]so the $x$-coordinate of $Q$ is
\[\frac{y_Q^2}{4a} = 2a (20a - 2t + \frac{1}{25} t^2) = 40a^2 - 4at + \frac{2}{25} t^2.\]The $x$-coordinate of $R$ is simply $t.$  Hence, the intersection of line $QR$ with the $x$-axis is
\[\boxed{40a^2}.\]

In general, if $\log_{12} a, \log_{12}  b, \log_{12}  c$ is an arithmetic progression, then we have \[2\log _{12} b = \log_{12} a + \log_{12}  c,\]or $\log_{12}  b^2 = \log_{12}  ac.$ Thus, $b^2 = ac,$ which means that $a, b, c$ is a geometric progression.

In our case, we see that $162, x, y, z, 1250$ must be a geometric progression. If $r$ is the common ratio, then we have $162r^4 = 1250,$ so $r^4 = \frac{1250}{162} = \frac{625}{81} = \frac{5^4}{3^4},$ and $r = \frac{5}{3}.$ (Note that $x, y, z$ must be positive for the logs to be defined, so $r$ must be positive as well.) Then \[x = 162 \cdot \frac{5}{3} = \boxed{270}.\]

Answer: We have \begin{align*}
\log_{12}{x}-\log_{12}{162}&=\log_{12}{z}-\log_{12}{y} \\
\frac{\log{x}}{\log{12}}-\frac{\log{162}}{\log{12}}&=\frac{\log{z}}{\log{12}}-\frac{\log{y}}{\log{12}} \\
\frac{\log{x}-\log{162}}{\log{12}}&=\frac{\log{z}-\log{y}}{\log{12}} \\
\log_{162}{x}&=\log_{y}{z}.
\end{align*}Therefore, $x = 162^{\log_y z}$. We can find $z$ by noting that $1250=12^3+\dots+12^0$, so \[\log_{12}{1250}=\frac{\log{1250}}{\log{12}}=\frac{\log{(12^3+\dots+12^0)}}{\log{12}}=3-\frac{1}{1+\dots+1/12}\]where there are $4$ terms in the denominator. Hence $\log_{12}{z}=3-\frac{1}{1+\dots+1/12}+\frac{4}{12}$. Also, $\log_{12}{y}=\log_{12}{162}-(3-\frac{1}{1+\dots+1/12})+\frac{2}{12}$. Therefore, we have \[x=162^{3-\frac{1}{1+\dots+1/12}+\frac{4}{12}}=162\cdot162^{2-\frac{1}{1+\dots+1/12}}\]and \[y=162^{3-\frac{1}{1+\dots+1/12}+\frac{2}{12}}=162\cdot162^{2-\frac{1}{1+\dots+1/12}}.\]It follows that $x/y=\boxed{125}$, since the $162\cdot162^{2-\frac{1}{1+\dots+1/12}}$ terms cancel out.

\[r_1 + r_2 + r_3 + r_4 = \frac{1}{2} + 1 + \frac{5}{4} + 2 = \frac{19}{4}.\]So by Vieta's formulas, $a = \boxed{19}.$

Vieta's formulas give us
\begin{align*}
\frac{a}{4} &= r_1 + r_2 + r_3 + r_4, \\
\frac{b}{4} &= r_1 r_2 + r_1 r_3 + r_1 r_4 + r_2 r_3 + r_2 r_4 + r_3 r_4, \\
\frac{c}{4} &= r_1 r_2 r_3 + r_1 r_2 r_4 + r_1 r_3 r_4 + r_2 r_3 r_4, \\
\frac{5}{4} &= r_1 r_2 r_3 r_4.
\end{align*}Then
\[1 = \frac{r_1}{2} + \frac{r_2}{4} + \frac{r_3}{5} + \frac{r_4}{8} < \frac{r_1}{2} + \frac{r_2}{4} + \frac{r_3}{4} + \frac{r_4}{4} = \frac{1}{2} (r_1 + r_2 + r_3 + r_4) = \frac{a}{8},\]so $a > 32.$

Also,
\[r_1 + r_2 + r_3 + r_4 = \frac{a}{4} > 8,\]so not all of the $r_i$ can be equal.  Also, we cannot have $r_1 \ge 2.$  Then $r_2 < 2,$ so
\[1 = \frac{r_1}{2} + \frac{r_2}{4} + \frac{r_3}{5} + \frac{r_4}{8} < \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{2} + \frac{r_3}{5} + \frac{r_4}{8} = \frac{1}{2} \left( 1 + \frac{8r_3 + 5r_4}{40} \right).\]Hence, $8r_3 + 5r_4 > 72.$  However, since $r_3,$ $r_4 > 0,$ we must have $r_1,$ $r_2 < 2$ and $r_3,$ $r_4 < 3.$  Then $r_1 + r_2 + r_3 + r_4 < 10,$ contradiction.

Hence, $a = \boxed{33}.$

f(-x) &= \frac{1}{-x + 2} - \frac{1}{-x - 2} \\

\[f(x) = \frac{1}{x + 2} - \frac{1}{x - 2} = \frac{(x - 2) - (x + 2)}{x^2 - 4} = -\frac{4}{x^2 - 4}.\]In this form, it is clear that $f(x)$ is even.

First, we can take out a factor of $z,$ to get
\[z(z^4 + z^3 + 2z^2 + z + 1) = 0.\]We can write $z^4 + z^3 + 2z^2 + z + 1 = 0$ as
\[(z^4 + z^3 + z^2) + (z^2 + z + 1) = z^2 (z^2 + z + 1) + (z^2 + z + 1) = (z^2 + 1)(z^2 + z + 1) = 0.\]If $z = 0,$ then $|z| = 0.$

If $z^2 + 1 = 0,$ then $z^2 = -1.$  Taking the absolute value of both sides, we get $|z^2| = 1.$  Then
\[|z|^2 = 1,\]so $|z| = 1.$  (Also, the roots of $z^2 + 1 = 0$ are $z = \pm i,$ both of which have absolute value 1.)

If $z^2 + z + 1 = 0,$ then $(z - 1)(z^2 + z + 1) = 0,$ which expands as $z^3 - 1 = 0.$  Then $z^3 = 1.$  Taking the absolute value of both sides, we get
\[|z^3| = 1,\]so $|z|^3 = 1.$  Hence, $|z| = 1.$

Therefore, the possible values of $|z|$ are $\boxed{0,1}.$

From $z^5 + z^4 + 2z^3 + z^2 + z = 0,$ $z$ cannot be 0.  Hence, we can divide both sides by $z^3$ to get
\[z^2 + \frac{1}{z} + 2 + \frac{1}{z^2} + \frac{1}{z^3} = 0.\]Then $z^2 + \frac{1}{z^2} = -(2 + \frac{1}{z} + \frac{1}{z^3}).$

We can then write
\begin{align*}
\left( z + \frac{1}{z} + 1 \right)^2 &= z^2 + 2z \cdot \frac{1}{z} + 1 + 2z + \frac{2}{z} + \frac{1}{z^2} \\
&= z^2 + \frac{1}{z^2} + 2z + \frac{2}{z} + 2 \\
&= 2 - (2 + \frac{1}{z} + \frac{1}{z^3}) + 2z + \frac{2}{z}.
\end{align*}Hence,
\[\left( z + \frac{1}{z} + 1 \right)^2 = 4 - \frac{1}{z} - z - \frac{1}{z^3} - z^3 = 4 - \frac{1}{z} - \frac{1}{z^3} - z - z^3.\]Note that
\[\left| z + \frac{1}{z} \right| = \left| z^2 + 1 + z \cdot \frac{1}{z} \right| \ge |z^2 + 1| - |z| = |z^2 + 1| - 1.\]Then
\[\left| z + \frac{1}{z} + 1 \right| \ge |z^2 + 1|.\]Therefore,
\[|z^2 + 1| \le 4.\]Hence, $|z|^2 + 1 \le 4,$ so $|z| \le \sqrt{3}.$

Equality occurs when $z = \operatorname{cis} \frac{2 \pi}{3}$ or $z = \operatorname{cis} \frac{4 \pi}{3},$ so the possible values of $|z|$ are $\boxed{\sqrt{3}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
We have
$$f(x) = q(x)(x^2-1) + r(x),$$where $q(x)$ is the quotient and $r(x)$ is the remainder. Since $x^2-1$ is quadratic, the remainder is at most linear; let us write $r(x) = ax+b$.

Observe that $x=-1$ and $x=1$ are both zeroes of $x^2-1$. Thus $f(1)=r(1)$ and $f(-1)=r(-1)$.

We can use the given formula for $f(x)$ to compute $f(1)=-10$ and $f(-1)=16$. Thus we have the system of equations
$$\begin{cases}
-10 = a\cdot (1) + b,\\
\phantom{-}16 = a\cdot (-1) + b.
\end{cases}$$Adding these equations yields $6=2b$ and hence $b=3$. Substituting into either equation then yields $a=-13$.

Therefore, $r(x) = ax+b = \boxed{-13x+3}$.

Note that $z^2 + z + 1 = 0$ and $z^3 = 1.$  Also, note that $s_2 = zs_1$ and $s_3 = z^2 s_1.$

The sum of the coefficients of $g(x)$ is
\begin{align*}
g(1) &= (1 - s_1)(1 - s_2)(1 - s_3) \\
&= (1 - s_1)(1 - s_1 z)(1 - s_1 z^2) \\
&= 1 - (1 + z + z^2) s_1 + (z + z^2 + z^3) s_1^2 - z^3 s_1^3 \\
&= 1 - s_1^3.
\end{align*}We have that
\[s_1^3 = r_1^3 + r_2^3 + r_3^3 + 3r_1^2 r_2 z + 3r_1^2 r_3 z^2 + 3r_2^2 r_3 z + 3r_2^2 r_1 z^2 + 3r_3^2 r_1 z + 3r_3^2 r_2 z^2 + 6r_1 r_2 r_3.\]Note that $r_1,$ $r_2,$ and $r_3$ are all real, and the real part of both $z$ and $z^2$ are $-\frac{1}{2},$ so the real part of $s_1^3$ is
\begin{align*}
&r_1^3 + r_2^3 + r_3^3 - \frac{3}{2} (r_1^2 r_2 + r_1 r_2^2 + r_1^2 r_3 + r_1 r_3^2 + r_2^2 r_3 + r_2 r_3^2) + 6r_1 r_2 r_3 \\
&= (r_1 + r_2 + r_3)^3 - \frac{9}{2} (r_1 + r_2 + r_3)(r_1 r_2 + r_1 r_3 + r_2 r_3) + \frac{27}{2} r_1 r_2 r_3 \\
&=3^3 - \frac{9}{2} (3)(-4) + \frac{27}{2} (-4) = 27.
\end{align*}Hence, the real part of the sum of the coefficients of $g(x)$ is $1 - 27 = \boxed{-26}.$

By AM-GM,
\begin{align*}
\frac{x^4 + 1}{x} &= x^3 + \frac{1}{x} \\
&= x^3 + \frac{1}{3x} + \frac{1}{3x} + \frac{1}{3x} \\
&\ge 4 \sqrt[4]{x^3 \cdot \frac{1}{3x} \cdot \frac{1}{3x} \cdot \frac{1}{3x}} \\
&= \frac{4}{\sqrt[4]{27}}.
\end{align*}Similarly,
\[\frac{z^4 + 1}{z} \ge  \frac{4}{\sqrt[4]{27}}.\]Again by AM-GM,
\[\frac{y^4 + 1}{y^2} = y^2 + \frac{1}{y^2} \ge 2 \sqrt{y^2 \cdot \frac{1}{y^2}} = 2.\]Therefore,
\[\frac{(x^4 + 1)(y^4 + 1)(z^4 + 1)}{xy^2 z} \ge \frac{4}{\sqrt[4]{27}} \cdot 2 \cdot \frac{4}{\sqrt[4]{27}} = \frac{32 \sqrt{3}}{9}.\]Equality occurs when $x^3 = \frac{1}{3x},$ $y^2 = \frac{1}{y^2},$ and $z^3 = \frac{1}{3z}.$  We can solve, to get $x = \frac{1}{\sqrt[4]{3}},$ $y = 1,$ and $z = \frac{1}{\sqrt[4]{3}},$ so the minimum value is $\frac{32 \sqrt{3}}{9}.$  The final answer is $32 + 3 + 9 = \boxed{44}.$

\begin{align*}

\begin{align*}

\end{align*}Equality occurs when $x = y = z,$ so the minimum value is $\boxed{705}.$

We first simplify the expressions for $f(x)$ and $g(x).$ We have \[ f(x) = \frac{1}{x + \frac1x} = \frac{1}{ \tfrac{x^2+1}{x} } = \frac{x}{x^2+1} \]and \[ g(x) = \frac{1}{x-\frac1x} = \frac{1}{\tfrac{x^2-1}{x}} = \frac{x}{x^2-1}.\]Therefore, \[\begin{aligned} (g(x))^2 - (f(x))^2 &= \frac{x^2}{(x^2-1)^2} - \frac{x^2}{(x^2+1)^2} \\ &= \frac{x^2\left((x^2+1)^2-(x^2-1)^2\right)}{(x^2-1)^2(x^2+1)^2} \\ &= \frac{x^2(4x^2)}{(x^4-1)^2} \\ &= \frac{4x^4}{(x^4-1)^2}. \end{aligned}\]Therefore, we have the equation \[\frac{4x^4}{(x^4-1)^2} = \frac{5}{4}.\]Cross-multiplying and expanding, we get \[16x^4 = 5x^8 - 10x^4 + 5,\]so \[0 = 5x^8 - 26x^4 + 5.\]This factors as \[0 = (5x^4-1)(x^4-5),\]so either $x^4 = \tfrac15$ or $x^4 = 5.$ It follows that the largest solution to the equation is $x = \sqrt[4]{5},$ so $x^2 = \boxed{\sqrt5}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Set $y=f(x)$ and $z=g(x).$ Substituting $y$ and $z$ into $(g(x))^2 - (f(x))^2 = \tfrac54$ and cross-multiplying, we get \[5z^2-5y^2=4y^2z^2-4.\]We take cases depending on the sign of $z$:
[list][*]If $z\leq0,$ then $y\leq0,$ so $5z^2-5y^2\geq0,$ a contradiction.
[*]If $0<z\leq1,$ then $y\geq1,$ which means $5z^2-5y^2\leq-5<0,$ contradiction again.
[*]If $1<z,$ then \[5z^2>5y^2+4y^2z^2-4\geq0,\]so $5z^2-4y^2z^2>4.$ Applying AM-GM to $y-\frac{1}{y}$ and $z+\frac{1}{z},$ we get \[y-\tfrac{1}{y}+z+\tfrac{1}{z}\geq2\sqrt{\left(y-\tfrac{1}{y}\right)\left(z+\tfrac{1}{z}\right)}=2\sqrt{(yz-1)^2+(y+z)^2}\geq2\sqrt2.\]We handle the inequality $y\leq\frac{1}{z}.$ Substituting $y$ and $z$ with $t$ and $t'$ respectively, we want to find the minimum of $t+\frac{1}{t'}$ over $t>1$ and $t'>1.$ Differentiating with $t,$ we get that this minimum is achieved at $t=t',$ so $t=\sqrt[3]{\frac{3}{2}}\implies t+\frac{1}{t'}=\frac{17}{\sqrt[3]{54}}.$ Thus, the largest possible $z$ is $\frac{17\sqrt3}{18}$, so the largest possible $x$ is $\frac{1+\sqrt{221}}{4},$ and our answer is $\left(\frac{1+\sqrt{221}}{4}\right)^2=\boxed{\frac{1861}{16}}.$[/list]

We have an arithmetico-geometric series with common ratio $\frac{1}{3}$. Let the sum be $S$. When we multiply by $\frac{1}{3}$ we get
$$\frac{S}{3} = \frac{1}{3} + \frac{3}{9} + \frac{5}{27} + \frac{7}{81} + \dotsb$$Subtracting this from the original series gives us
$$\begin{aligned} \frac{2}{3}S &= 1+\frac{2}{3} + \frac{2}{9} + \frac{2}{27} + \frac{2}{81} + \dotsb \\
&= 1 + \frac{\frac{2}{3}}{1-\frac{1}{3}} = 1+ 1 = 2.
\end{aligned}$$Then $S = \boxed{3}$.

Note that the sum of all numbers in the 3 by 3 square is $25 + 21 + 18 + 24 + v + w + x + y + z = 135$. Therefore, since the sum of each row, column, and diagonal is equal, each must be $\frac{1}{3} \cdot 135 = 45$. 

The sum of the first row is $25+z+21=46+z$, the sum of the second row is $18+x+y$, and the sum of the third row is $v+24+w$. Since these all must be equal, we have $46+z = 18+x+y = v+24+w$. 

Adding the second and third equations, we have $18+x+y+v+24+w = 2(y+z+21)$. Substituting in $46+z$ for the first equation, we have $46+z+v+24+w = 2(y+z+21)$, which simplifies to $y+z = \boxed{44}$.

We can write the equation as $x^4 = 2x^2 + 7.$

If $x$ is negative, then $x^4$ is positive, but $2x^2 + 7$ is negative, so there are no negative real roots.

If $x = 0,$ then $x^4 = 0,$ so there is exactly $\boxed{1}$ real root.

If $x$ is positive, then we can write the equation as
\[\left( x^2 - \sqrt{2 + \sqrt{7}} \right) \left( x^2 + \sqrt{2 + \sqrt{7}} \right) = 0.\]Since $x^2 + \sqrt{2 + \sqrt{7}}$ is always positive, the only possible positive roots come when $x^2 - \sqrt{2 + \sqrt{7}} = 0,$ or $x^2 = \sqrt{2 + \sqrt{7}}.$  This equation has exactly one positive solution.

After a bit of algebra, we obtain:
\[h(z)=g(g(z))=f(f(f(f(z))))=\frac{Pz+Q}{Rz+S},\]where $P=(a+1)^2+a(b+1)^2$, $Q=a(b+1)(b^2+2a+1)$, $R=(b+1)(b^2+2a+1)$, and $S=a(b+1)^2+(a+b^2)^2$. In order for $h(z)=z$, we must have $R=0$, $Q=0$, and $P=S$. The first implies $b=-1$ or $b^2+2a+1=0$. The second implies $a=0$, $b=-1$, or $b^2+2a+1=0$. The third implies $b=\pm1$ or $b^2+2a+1=0$.

Since $|a|=1\neq 0$, in order to satisfy all 3 conditions we must have either $b=1$ or $b^2+2a+1=0$. In the first case $|b|=1$. For the latter case, note that $|b^2+1|=|-2a|=2$, so $2=|b^2+1|\leq |b^2|+1$ and hence $1\leq|b|^2\Rightarrow1\leq |b|$. On the other hand, $2=|b^2+1|\geq|b^2|-1$, so $|b^2|\leq 3\Rightarrow0\leq |b|\leq \sqrt{3}$.

Thus, $1\leq |b|\leq \sqrt{3}$. Hence, in any case the maximum value for $|b|$ is $\sqrt{3}$ while the minimum is $1$ (which can be achieved in the instance where $|a|=1,|b|=\sqrt{3}$ or $|a|=1,|b|=1$ respectively). The answer is then $\boxed{\sqrt{3}-1}$.

GPT File: datasets/persona_short_answer/persona_short_answer/intermediate_algebra/1386_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
$g(g(z)) = z$ means that $g(z)$ is an involution, i.e. $g(g(z)) = z$ for every $z$ in its domain. Thus $g(z)$ must have either 1 or 2 fixed points. You can verify that $g(z)$ has a fixed point by solving for $z$ in $g(z) = z$. \[f(f(z)) = g(z) = z \qquad \Rightarrow \qquad f(f(z))-z = \frac{(a-b) z - ab}{b(z+b)}\] Thus, $g(z)$ has exactly one fixed point (a root to the above equation) if $\left| \frac{ab}{a-b} \right| \neq 1$ and exactly two fixed points if $\left| \frac{ab}{a-b} \right| = 1$. Since $|a|=1$, assume $a=\operatorname{cis}(\theta)$ for some real $\theta$ (and $b$ is real).

In the case of one fixed point:
The fixed point solves
\[f(f(z))=z \quad \Leftrightarrow \quad f(z)=z \quad \Leftrightarrow \quad z^2+z(a-b)+ab =0\] The quadratic formula gives
\[z = \frac{-(a-b)\pm\sqrt{(a-b)^2-4ab}}{2}\] Since $b$ is real, the discriminant is a positive real number, and so $z$ is either in the unit circle or outside it. Given that the one fixed point is, say, inside the unit circle, the corresponding $z$ is in the form
\[z = \frac{-(a-b)+\sqrt{(a-b)^2-4ab}}{2}\] By forward computation, we get $b = -\tfrac{2}{e^{2i\theta}-1}$. We can easily compute the range of $b$ for this case.

In the case of two fixed points:
There exist $w$ and $z$ such that $g(w)=w$ and $g(z)=z$ with $w$ inside the unit circle and $z$ outside of it. Since $\left|\tfrac{ab}{a-b}\right|=1$, we must have that $w+z=-(a+b)$ and $wz = ab$ by Vieta's Formulas. By forward computation, we have that
\[b=\frac{(1+z)(1+w)}{zw} = -\frac{2}{e^{2i\theta}+1}\] Again, we can easily compute the range of $b$ in this case.

Putting it all together, the difference between the maximum and minimum possible values of $|b|$ is $\boxed{\tfrac{4}{3}}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Setting $x = y = 0,$ we get
\[2f(0) = f(0) - 1,\]so $f(0) = -1.$

Setting $y = 1,$ we get
\[f(x) + 1 = f(x + 1) - x - 1,\]so
\[f(x + 1) - f(x) = x + 2.\]Thus,
\begin{align*}
f(2) - f(1) &= 1 + 2, \\
f(3) - f(2) &= 2 + 2, \\
f(4) - f(3) &= 3 + 2, \\
&\dots, \\
f(n) - f(n - 1) &= (n - 1) + 2.
\end{align*}Adding all the equations, we get
\[f(n) - f(1) = 1 + 2 + 3 + \dots + (n - 1) + 2(n - 1) = \frac{(n - 1)n}{2} + 2n - 2 = \frac{n^2 + 3n - 4}{2},\]so
\[f(n) = \frac{n^2 + 3n - 2}{2}\]for all positive integers $n.$

Setting $x = -n$ and $y = n,$ where $n$ is a positive integer, we get
\[f(-n) + f(n) = f(0) + n^2 - 1.\]Then
\[f(-n) = n^2 - f(n) + f(0) - 1 = n^2 - \frac{n^2 + 3n - 2}{2} - 2 = \frac{n^2 - 3n - 2}{2}.\]Thus, the formula
\[f(n) = \frac{n^2 + 3n - 2}{2}\]holds for all integers $n.$

We want to solve $f(n) = n,$ or
\[\frac{n^2 + 3n - 2}{2} = n.\]Then $n^2 + 3n - 2 = 2n,$ or $n^2 + n - 2 = 0.$  This factors as $(n - 1)(n + 2) = 0,$ so the solutions are $n = \boxed{1,-2}.$

Let $n$ be an integer.  Setting $x = y = \frac{n}{2},$ we get
\[2f \left( \frac{n}{2} \right) = f(n) - \frac{n^2}{4} - 1.\]Therefore,
\[f(n) = 2f \left( \frac{n}{2} \right) + \frac{n^2}{2} + 1.\]Repeating the process, we get
\[f(n) = 2^k f \left( \frac{n}{2^k} \right) + \frac{n^2}{2^k} + (2^k - 1).\]Take $k$ such that $2^k$ is odd and greater than $n,$ or $k = \left\lceil \log_2 (n + 1) \right\rceil.$  Then
\[f(n) = 2^k f \left( \frac{n}{2^k} \right) + \frac{n^2}{2^k} + (2^k - 1) = n,\]so $f(n) = n$ for $n = -1,$ $0,$ $1.$

We claim that $f(n) = n$ for all integers $n.$  Assume that $f(k) = k$ for all integers $k$ such that $-1 \le k \le n - 1.$  We want to show that $f(n) = n.$

Let $x = n - a$ and $y = a$ for some positive integer $a.$  Then
\[f(n) + f(a) = f(n - a) - a(n - a) - 1 + f(a),\]so
\[f(n) = f(n - a) - a(n - a) - 1.\]Setting $a = \left\lceil \sqrt{n + 1} \right\rceil,$ we get
\[f(n) = f \left( \left\lceil n - \left\lceil \sqrt{n + 1} \right\rceil + 1 \right\rceil \right) - \left\lceil \sqrt{n + 1} \right\rceil (n - \left\lceil \sqrt{n + 1} \right\rceil + 1) - 1.\]Note that $0 \le \left\lceil n - \left\lceil \sqrt{n + 1} \right\rceil + 1 \right\rceil - 1 \le \sqrt{n + 1}.$  Thus, we can write
\[f(n) = f(m) - k,\]where $m = \left\lceil n - \left\lceil \sqrt{n + 1} \right\rceil + 1 \right\rceil$ and $k = \left\lceil \sqrt{n + 1} \right\rceil (n - \left\lceil \sqrt{n + 1} \right\rceil + 1) + 1.$

Then $m \le n,$ so $k \le \left\lfloor (n + 1) - \sqrt{n + 1} \right\rfloor + 1 < 2 \sqrt{n + 1}.$

By the induction hypothesis, $f(m) = m,$ so
\[f(n) = m - k.\]We want to show that $f(n) = n,$ or $m - k = n.$  This will follow if $2 \sqrt{n + 1} \le m - n,$ or
\[m \ge n + 2 \sqrt{n + 1} + 1.\]By definition of $m,$
\[m \le n - \left\lceil \sqrt{n + 1} \right\rceil + 2 < n - \sqrt{n + 1} + 3.\]we want to show that
\[n - \left\lceil \sqrt{n + 1} \right\rceil + 2 \ge n + 2 \sqrt{n + 1} + 1.\]This reduces to $\left\lceil \sqrt{n + 1} \right\rceil \le \sqrt{n + 1} - 1,$ which holds.

Therefore, $f(n) = n$ for all integers $n,$ and the solutions are $\boxed{-1,0,1}.$

Since the remainder is $2x + 1$ when $f(x)$ is divided by $(x - 1)^2,$ we can write
\begin{align*}
f(x) &= q(x) (x - 1)^2 + 2x + 1 \\
&= q(x) (x - 1)^2 + 2(x - 1) + 3.
\end{align*}Then
\[\frac{f(x) - 3}{x - 1} = q(x)(x - 1) + 2.\]Let
\[g(x) = q(x)(x - 1) + 2.\]By the Remainder Theorem, $f(3) = 15,$ so
\[g(3) = \frac{f(3) - 3}{3 - 1} = \frac{15 - 3}{3 - 1} = 6.\]Also, $g(1) = 2.$

Let $ax + b$ be the remainder when $g(x)$ is divided by $(x - 1)(x - 3),$ so
\[g(x) = q_1(x) (x - 1)(x - 3) + ax + b.\]Setting $x = 1$ and $x = 3$ gives
\begin{align*}
a + b &= g(1) = 2, \\
3a + b &= g(3) = 6.
\end{align*}Solving this system, we get $a = 2$ and $b = 0,$ so
\[g(x) = q_1(x)(x - 1)(x - 3) + 2x.\]Then
\begin{align*}
f(x) &= g(x) (x - 1) + 3 \\
&= [q_1(x) (x - 1)(x - 3) + 2x](x - 1) + 3 \\
&= q_1(x) (x - 1)^2 (x - 3) + 2x(x - 1) + 3 \\
&= q_1(x) (x - 1)^2 (x - 3) + 2x^2 - 2x + 3.
\end{align*}This tells us that the remainder when $f(x)$ is divided by $(x - 3)(x - 1)^2$ is $\boxed{2x^2 - 2x + 3}.$

\end{align*}so the remainder is $\boxed{\frac{8}{2} + 5 - \frac{31}{2} = \frac{10}{2}} = \boxed{5}.$

Let $a$ and $b$ be any real numbers.  Then by the Trivial Inequality,
\[(a - b)^2 \ge 0.\]This expands as $a^2 - 2ab + b^2 \ge 0,$ so
\[a^2 + b^2 \ge 2ab.\](This looks like AM-GM, but we want an inequality that works with all real numbers.)

Setting $a = 2xy$ and $b = x^2 - y^2,$ we get
\[(2xy)^2 + (x^2 - y^2)^2 \ge 2(2xy)(x^2 - y^2).\]The left-hand side simplifies to $(x^2 + y^2)^2.$  From the given equation,
\[2(2xy)(x^2 - y^2) = 4(xy)(x^2 - y^2) = 4(x^2 + y^2),\]so $(x^2 + y^2)^2 \ge 4(x^2 + y^2).$  Since both $x$ and $y$ are nonzero, $x^2 + y^2 > 0,$ so we can divide both sides by $x^2 + y^2$ to get
\[x^2 + y^2 \ge 4.\]Equality occurs only when $2xy = x^2 - y^2,$ or $y^2 + 2xy - x^2 = 0.$  By the quadratic formula,
\[y = \frac{-2 \pm \sqrt{4 - 4(1)(-1)}}{2} \cdot x = (-1 \pm \sqrt{2})x.\]Suppose $y = (-1 + \sqrt{2})x.$  Substituting into $x^2 + y^2 = 4,$ we get
\[x^2 + (1 - 2 \sqrt{2} + 2) x^2 = 4.\]Then $(4 - 2 \sqrt{2}) x^2 = 4,$ so
\[x^2 = \frac{4}{4 - 2 \sqrt{2}} = 2 + \sqrt{2}.\]So equality occurs, for instance, when $x = \sqrt{2 + \sqrt{2}}$ and $y = (-1 + \sqrt{2}) \sqrt{2 + \sqrt{2}}.$  We conclude that the minimum value is $\boxed{4}.$

We have that
\[x^3 y - xy^3 = \frac{x^2 + y^2}{x^2 - y^2}.\]Let $a = x^2$ and $b = y^2.$  Then $ab(a - b) = a + b,$ and we want to minimize $a + b.$  From $ab(a - b) = a + b,$
\[a - b = \frac{a + b}{ab}.\]Let $t = \frac{a + b}{2}.$  Then $a = t + d$ and $b = t - d$ for some $d,$ so
\[\frac{2d}{(t + d)(t - d)} = \frac{1}{t}.\]Then
\[d^2 + t^2 = t^2 - d^2,\]so $d = 0.$  Hence, $a = b,$ so $2a^3 = 2a,$ which means $a = 1.$

Thus, the minimum value of $x^2 + y^2$ is $\boxed{2}.$  This can be achieved with $x = 1$ and $y = 1.$

Let $p(x) = ax^2 + bx + c.$  Then
\begin{align*}
7a + b \sqrt{7} + c &= 22, \\
11a + b \sqrt{11} + c &= 30.
\end{align*}Since $a,$ $b,$ and $c$ are rational, the only way that the equations above can hold is if $b = 0.$  Then
\begin{align*}
7a + c &= 22, \\

\begin{align*}
(\sqrt{7})^2 + (-\sqrt{7})^2 &= -\frac{b}{a}, \\
(\sqrt{11})^2 + (-\sqrt{11})^2 &= -\frac{b}{a}.
\end{align*}Hence, $b = 0.$

\[p(x) = a(x^2 - k^2) = a(x + k)(x - k)\]for some $k.$  Then $22 = p(\sqrt{7}) = a(\sqrt{7} + k)(\sqrt{7} - k)$ and $30 = p(\sqrt{11}) = a(\sqrt{11} + k)(\sqrt{11} - k).$  Dividing these equations, we get

We can write
\[x^2 + y^2 + 2xy - 14x - 14y + 49 = (x + y - 7)^2 + y^2 - 18y + 68.\]Let $t = y - 3.$  Then the given equation becomes
\[3x^2 - 18x + 4(t + 3)^2 - 32(t + 3) + 91 = 300.\]Simplifying, we get $3x^2 - 18x + 4t^2 - 32t + 10 = 0,$ or $3x^2 - 18x = -4t^2 + 32t - 10.$  Then
\[(x + y - 7)^2 + y^2 - 18y + 68 = (x + y - 7)^2 + (y - 3)^2 + 59 - 18y.\]Thus, we want to maximize $(x + y - 7)^2 + (y - 3)^2 - 18y.$

We can write
\[x + y - 7 = -\frac{3x^2 - 18x + 4y^2 - 32y + 91 - 300}{3x - 18},\]so
\[(x + y - 7)^2 + (y - 3)^2 - 18y = -\frac{(3x - 2y - 11)^2 + 235}{3x - 18}.\]Thus, we want to maximize
\[-\frac{(3x - 2y - 11)^2 + 235}{3x - 18}.\]Note that $3x - 18 \neq 0,$ so we can ignore the denominator.  We want to minimize $(3x - 2y - 11)^2.$

Let $u = 3x - 2y$ and $v = 2x + 3y.$  Then $u^2 + v^2$ is invariant under rotations, so we can take $u = r \cos \theta$ and $v = r \sin \theta.$  Then $x = \frac{2}{13} u + \frac{3}{13} v$ and $y = -\frac{3}{13} u + \frac{2}{13} v.$

Let $f(x,y) = x^2 + y^2 + 2xy - 14x - 14y + 49.$  Then
\begin{align*}
f \left( \frac{2}{13} u + \frac{3}{13} v, -\frac{3}{13} u + \frac{2}{13} v \right) &= \left( \frac{2}{13} u + \frac{3}{13} v - \frac{21}{13} \right)^2 \\
&\quad + \left( -\frac{3}{13} u + \frac{2}{13} v - 3 \right)^2 + 59 \\
&= \frac{13}{2} r^2 \sin^2 \left( \theta + \frac{53 \pi}{180} \right) + 59.
\end{align*}This is maximized when $\sin \left( \theta + \frac{53 \pi}{180} \right) = 1,$ or $\theta = 67^\circ.$  Thus, the maximum value is $\boxed{\frac{941}{2}}.$

We can rewrite this as two separate series
$$\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \left( \frac{2n}{3^n} - \frac{1}{2^n} \right) = 2\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{n}{3^n} - \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{2^n}.$$The first, $S = \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{n}{3^n} = \frac{1}{3} +  \frac{2}{9} + \frac{3}{27} + \dotsb$ is an arithmetico-geometric series. Multipling by 3, the inverse of the common ratio, gives us
$$3S = \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{n}{3^{n-1}} = 1+ \frac{2}{3} +  \frac{3}{9} + \frac{4}{27} + \dotsb$$Subtracting $S$ from $3S$ gives
$$\begin{aligned} 2S &=  1+ \frac{1}{3} +  \frac{1}{9} + \frac{1}{27} + \dotsb \\
&= \frac{1}{1-\frac{1}{3}} \\
&= \frac{3}{2}.
\end{aligned}$$The second series is a geometric series so we have
$$\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{2^n} = \frac{\frac{1}{2}}{1-\frac{1}{2}} = 1.$$Hence,
$$2\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{n}{3^n} - \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{2^n} = \frac{3}{2} - 1 = \boxed{\frac{1}{2}}.$$

First, note that the series organizes as $$\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{2n}{3^n} - \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{2^n}.$$We apply the formula for a geometric series to both series on the right-hand side: $$\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{2n}{3^n} = \frac{\frac{2}{3}}{1 - \frac{1}{3}} \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{2}{3^{n-1}} = \frac{2}{\frac{2}{3}} = 3$$and $$\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{2^n} = \frac{1}{1-\frac{1}{2}} = 2.$$Therefore, the sum we seek is $3-2=\boxed{1}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
We complete the square:
\begin{align*}
|z + w|^2 &= (z + w)(\overline{z + w}) \\
&= (z + w)(\overline{z} + \overline{w}) \\
&= zz + zw + \overline{z} w + ww \\
&= |z|^2 + |w|^2 + z \overline{w} + \overline{z} w \\
&= 4 + 25 + z \overline{w} + \overline{z} w \\
&= 29 + 2 \operatorname{Re} (z \overline{w}).
\end{align*}Note that $|z + w|^2$ is maximized when $z$ and $w$ are parallel.  Since $|z| = 2$ and $|w| = 5,$
\[zw = |z||w| e^{i \theta} = 10 e^{i \theta}\]for some angle $\theta.$

Then
\[z \overline{w} + \overline{z} w = 2 \operatorname{Re} (z \overline{w}) = 2 \operatorname{Re} (z \cdot \frac{10}{z}) = 20.\]Hence,
\[|z + w|^2 = 29 + 2 \cdot 20 = 69,\]so $|z + w| = \boxed{\sqrt{69}}.$

Let $z = a + bi.$  Then
\[a + bi + \sqrt{a^2 + b^2} = 2 + 8i.\]Equating the imaginary parts, we get $b = 8.$  Equating the real parts, we get $a + \sqrt{a^2 + 64} = 2.$  Then
\[\sqrt{a^2 + 64} = 2 - a.\]Squaring both sides, we get $a^2 + 64 = (2 - a)^2 = a^2 - 4a + 4.$  Solving, we find $a = -15.$  Therefore, $z = \boxed{-15 + 8i}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
We can write $z = x + yi,$ where $x$ and $y$ are real numbers.  Then $|z| = \sqrt{x^2 + y^2},$ so the equation $z + |z| = 2 + 8i$ becomes
\[x + yi + \sqrt{x^2 + y^2} = 2 + 8i.\]But since $x$ and $y$ are real, $\sqrt{x^2 + y^2}$ is also real, so we can equate real and imaginary parts to get
\begin{align*}
x + \sqrt{x^2 + y^2} &= 2, \\
y &= 8.
\end{align*}Squaring the first equation, we get
\[x^2 + x^2 + y^2 + 2x \sqrt{x^2 + y^2} = 4.\]Substituting $y = 8,$ we get
\[2x \sqrt{x^2 + 64} = -12x^2 + 20,\]so $x \sqrt{x^2 + 64} = -6x^2 + 10.$  Squaring both sides, we get
\[x^4 - 16x^2 + 64x^2 = 36x^4 - 120x^2 + 100.\]This simplifies to $35x^4 - 104x^2 + 100 = 0,$ which factors as $(5x^2 - 4)(7x^2 - 25) = 0.$  Hence, $x^2 = \frac{4}{5}$ or $x^2 = \frac{25}{7}.$

If $x^2 = \frac{4}{5},$ then $x = \pm \frac{2}{\sqrt{5}},$ so
\[z = \frac{2}{\sqrt{5}} + 8i\]or $z = -\frac{2}{\sqrt{5}} + 8i.$

If $x^2 = \frac{25}{7},$ then $x = \pm \frac{5}{\sqrt{7}},$ so
\[z = \frac{5}{\sqrt{7}} + 8i\]or $z = -\frac{5}{\sqrt{7}} + 8i.$

Therefore, one solution is $z = \boxed{-\frac{2}{\sqrt{5}} + 8i}.$

Let $A = (a,a^2)$ and $C = (c,c^2).$  Since $\overline{OA}$ and $\overline{OC}$ are perpendicular, the product of their slopes is $-1$:
\[\frac{a^2}{a} \cdot \frac{c^2}{c} = -1.\]Thus, $ac = -1.$

[asy]
unitsize(2 cm);

real func (real x) {
  return(x^2);
}

pair A, B, C, O;

O = (0,0);
A = (0.8,func(0.8));
C = (-1/0.8,func(-1/0.8));
B = A + C - O;

draw(graph(func,-1.6,1.6));
draw(O--A--B--C--cycle);

dot("$A = (a,a^2)$", A, SE);
dot("$B$", B, N);
dot("$C = (c,c^2)$", C, SW);
dot("$O$", O, S);
[/asy]

As a rectangle, the midpoints of the diagonals coincide.  The midpoint of $\overline{AC}$ is
\[\left( \frac{a + c}{2}, \frac{a^2 + c^2}{2} \right),\]so $B = (a + c,a^2 + c^2).$

Let $x = a + c$ and $y = a^2 + c^2.$  We want a relationship between $x$ and $y$ in the form of $y = px^2 + qx + r.$  We have that
\[x^2 = (a + c)^2 = a^2 + 2ac + c^2 = a^2 + c^2 - 2 = y - 2,\]so the fixed parabola is $\boxed{y = x^2 + 2}.$

Consider the reflection of $A$ about the $y$-axis (which is point $A'$), and the reflection of $C$ about the $y$-axis (which is point $C'$).  Then $A'$ and $C'$ also lie on the parabola $y = x^2,$ and triangle $OA'C'$ is congruent to triangle $OAC.$



[asy]
unitsize(1.5 cm);

pair A, B, C, O;

A = (-1.5,2.25);
B = (-1.5,-2.25);
C = (1.5,-2.25);
O = (0,0);

draw(reflect((0,0),(1,0))*parabola(2.5),red);
draw((-2,0)--(2,0));
draw(graph(parabola, -1.5,1.5),red);
draw((-2,-3)--(-2,3));
draw(reflect((0,0),(1,0))*A--O--reflect((0,0),(1,0))*C);
draw(reflect((0,0),(1,0))*A--reflect((0,0),(1,0))*O--O--reflect((0,0),(1,0))*C);

label("$y = x^2$", (2.5,6), red);

dot("$A$", A, NW);
dot("$B$", B, SE);
dot("$C$", C, SE);
dot("$O$", O, N);
[/asy]

Since $A'B = BC',$ the parabola that passes through $A',$ $B,$ and $C'$ is symmetric about the $y$-axis.  This is the intersection of two parabolas $y = x^2$ and $x = \frac{y^2}{4}.$

Solving, we find $y^2 - 4x = 0$ and $x - y^2/4 = 0,$ so $y^4 - 16x = 0,$ or
\[y^4 = 16x.\]Hence, the fixed parabola is $\boxed{y = 4\sqrt[4]{x}}.$

We have that
\begin{align*}
z_1 \overline{z}_1 &= |z_1|^2, \\
z_2 \overline{z}_2 &= |z_2|^2, \\
z_3 \overline{z}_3 &= |z_3|^2.
\end{align*}Likewise,
\begin{align*}
&|z_1 - z_2|^2 + |z_1 - z_3|^2 + |z_2 - z_3|^2 \\
&= (z_1 - z_2)(\overline{z_1 - z_2}) + (z_1 - z_3)(\overline{z_1 - z_3}) + (z_2 - z_3)(\overline{z_2 - z_3}) \\
&= (z_1 - z_2)(\overline{z}_1 - \overline{z}_2) + (z_1 - z_3)(\overline{z}_1 - \overline{z}_3) + (z_2 - z_3)(\overline{z}_2 - \overline{z}_3) \\
&= z_1 \overline{z}_1 - z_1 \overline{z}_2 - \overline{z}_1 z_2 + z_2 \overline{z}_2 + z_1 \overline{z}_1 - z_1 \overline{z}_3 - \overline{z}_1 z_3 + z_1 \overline{z}_3 + z_2 \overline{z}_3 - z_2 \overline{z}_3 - \overline{z}_2 z_3 + z_2 \overline{z}_3 \\
&= 2|z_1|^2 + 2|z_2|^2 + 2|z_3|^2 - (z_1 \overline{z}_2 + \overline{z}_1 z_2 + z_1 \overline{z}_3 + \overline{z}_1 z_3 + z_2 \overline{z}_3 + \overline{z}_2 z_3).
\end{align*}Now,
\begin{align*}
|z_1 + z_2 + z_3|^2 &= (z_1 + z_2 + z_3)(\overline{z_1 + z_2 + z_3}) \\
&= (z_1 + z_2 + z_3)(\overline{z}_1 + \overline{z}_2 + \overline{z}_3) \\
&= z_1 \overline{z}_1 + z_1 \overline{z}_2 + z_1 \overline{z}_3 + z_2 \overline{z}_1 + z_2 \overline{z}_2 + z_2 \overline{z}_3 + z_3 \overline{z}_1 + z_3 \overline{z}_2 + z_3 \overline{z}_3 \\
&= |z_1|^2 + |z_2|^2 + |z_3|^2 + (z_1 \overline{z}_2 + \overline{z}_1 z_2 + z_1 \overline{z}_3 + \overline{z}_1 z_3 + z_2 \overline{z}_3 + \overline{z}_2 z_3).
\end{align*}Adding these two equations, we get
\[|z_1 - z_2|^2 + |z_1 - z_3|^2 + |z_2 - z_3|^2 + |z_1 + z_2 + z_3|^2 = 3|z_1|^2 + 3|z_2|^2 + 3|z_3|^2.\]Therefore,
\begin{align*}
|z_1 - z_2|^2 + |z_1 - z_3|^2 + |z_2 - z_3|^2 &= 3|z_1|^2 + 3|z_2|^2 + 3|z_3|^2 - |z_1 + z_2 + z_3|^2 \\
&\le 3 \cdot 2^2 + 3 \cdot 3^2 + 3 \cdot 4^2 \\
&= 87.
\end{align*}For equality to occur, we must have $z_1 + z_2 + z_3 = 0.$  Without loss of generality, we can assume that $z_1 = 2.$  Then $z_2 + z_3 = -2.$  Taking the conjugate, we get
\[\overline{z}_2 + \overline{z}_3 = -2.\]Since $|z_2| = 3,$ $\overline{z}_2 = \frac{9}{z_2}.$  Since $|z_3| = 4,$ $\overline{z}_3 = \frac{16}{z_3},$ so
\[\frac{9}{z_2} + \frac{16}{z_3} = -2.\]Then $9z_3 + 16z_2 = -2z_2 z_3.$  Substituting $z_3 = -z_2 - 2,$ we get
\[9(-z_2 - 2) + 16z_2 = -2z_2 (-z_2 - 2).\]This simplifies to $2z_2^2 - 3z_2 + 18 = 0.$  By the quadratic formula,
\[z_2 = \frac{3 \pm 3i \sqrt{15}}{4}.\]If we take $z_2 = \frac{3 + 3i \sqrt{15}}{4},$ then $z_3 = -\frac{11 + 3i \sqrt{15}}{4}.$  This example shows that equality is possible, so the maximum value is $\boxed{87}.$

[asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

pair zone, ztwo, zthree;

zone = (2,0);
ztwo = (3/4,3*sqrt(15)/4);
zthree = (-11/4,-3*sqrt(15)/4);

draw(Circle((0,0),2),red);
draw(Circle((0,0),3),green);
draw(Circle((0,0),4),blue);
draw(zone--ztwo--zthree--cycle);

dot("$z_1$", zone, E);
dot("$z_2$", ztwo, N);
dot("$z_3$", zthree, SW);
[/asy]

Alternative: For equality to occur, we must have $z_1 + z_2 + z_3 = 0.$  Without loss of generality, we can assume that $z_1 = 2.$  Then $z_2 + z_3 = -2.$  Let $z_2 = x + iy$ so that $z_3 = -x - 2 - iy,$ where $x$ and $y$ are real numbers.  We need
\begin{align*}
  |z_2|^2 = x^2 + y^2 &= 9 \\
  |z_3|^2 = (x + 2)^2 + y^2 &= 16.
\end{align*}Subtracting the first equation from the second, we get $4x + 4 = 7,$ or $x = \dfrac34.$  One solution is $z_2 = \dfrac34 + i\dfrac{3\sqrt{15}}{4}$ and $z_3 = -\dfrac{11}4 + i\dfrac{3\sqrt{15}}{4}.$  This example shows that equality is possible, so the maximum value is $\boxed{87}.$

We can write
\begin{align*}
|z_1 - z_2|^2 + |z_1 - z_3|^2 + |z_2 - z_3|^2 &= (z_1 - z_2) (\overline{z_1} - \overline{z_2}) + (z_1 - z_3)(\overline{z_1} - \overline{z_3}) + (z_2 - z_3)(\overline{z_2} - \overline{z_3}) \\
&= 3 \cdot (z_1 \overline{z_1} + z_2 \overline{z_2} + z_3 \overline{z_3}) - 2 \cdot (\overline{z_1} z_2 + \overline{z_2} z_1 + \overline{z_1} z_3 + \overline{z_3} z_1 + \overline{z_2} z_3 + \overline{z_3} z_2).
\end{align*}By the Triangle Inequality,
\begin{align*}
|\overline{z_1} z_2 + \overline{z_2} z_1| &= |\overline{z_1} z_2| + |\overline{z_2} z_1| \\
&\le |\overline{z_1}| \cdot |z_2| + |\overline{z_2}| \cdot |z_1| \\
&= 2 \cdot 3 + 3 \cdot 2 \\
&= 12,
\end{align*}and the other terms can be bounded similarly, to get
\[|z_1 - z_2|^2 + |z_1 - z_3|^2 + |z_2 - z_3|^2 \le 54.\]Equality occurs when $z_1 = 2,$ $z_2 = -3,$ and $z_3 = 6,$ so the largest possible value is $\boxed{54}.$

Since both roots are real, the discriminant must be nonnegative:
\[(-2k)^2 - 4(k^2 + k - 5) \ge 0.\]This simplifies to $20 - 4k \ge 0,$ so $k \le 5.$

Let
\[y = x^2 - 2kx + k^2 + k - 5 = (x - k)^2 + k - 5.\]Thus, parabola opens upward, and its vertex is $(k, k - 5).$  If $k = 5,$ then the quadratic has a double root of $x = 5,$ so we must have $k < 5.$  Then the vertex lies to the left of the line $x = 5.$

\[25 - 10k + k^2 + k - 5 > 0.\]Then $k^2 - 9k + 20 > 0,$ or $(k - 4)(k - 5) > 0.$  Since $k < 5,$ we must have $k < 4.$

Thus, both roots are less than 5 when $k \in \boxed{(-\infty,4)}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Complete the square to get
\[f(x) = (x + b)^2 + (1 - b^2),\]so the vertex of the parabola is $(-b,1-b^2).$  Also,
\[g(x) = 2a (x + b) = 2a x + 2ab,\]so $g(x)$ has a $y$-intercept of $(0,2ab).$

We claim that for a fixed $a,$ the graphs of $y = f(x)$ and $y = g(x)$ intersect if and only if $b^2 - ab + a^2 \ge \frac{1}{4}.$  Suppose $y = f(x)$ intersects $y = g(x)$ at $(x,y).$  Then
\begin{align*}
(x + b)^2 + (1 - b^2) &= 2ax + 2ab \\
\Rightarrow x^2 + (2b - 2a) x + (a^2 - 2b + 1 - b^2) &= 0.
\end{align*}By the quadratic formula,
\[x = \frac{2a - 2b \pm 2 \sqrt{(b - a)^2 - a^2 + b^2 - 1}}{2} = a - b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - ab + a^2 - \frac{1}{4}}.\]Since $(x,y)$ lies on both $y = f(x)$ and $y = g(x),$ $y = 2a(x + b) = 2a (a - b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - ab + a^2 - \frac{1}{4}} + b),$ so
\[2a^2 - 2ab \pm 2a \sqrt{b^2 - ab + a^2 - \frac{1}{4}} = 2ab - 2a^2 \mp 2a \sqrt{b^2 - ab + a^2 - \frac{1}{4}}.\]Hence,
\[2a^2 - 2ab = 2ab - 2a^2,\]so $a^2 = ab.$  We also see that
\[\pm 2a \sqrt{b^2 - ab + a^2 - \frac{1}{4}} = \pm 2a \sqrt{\frac{1}{4} + (a - b)^2 - a^2} = \pm a \sqrt{4a^2 - 4ab + 4b^2 - 1}.\]Thus, if the graphs of $y = f(x)$ and $y = g(x)$ intersect, then $a^2 = ab$ and
\[a \sqrt{4a^2 - 4ab + 4b^2 - 1} = -2a^2 + ab + ab - b^2 = a^2 - b^2,\]so
\[4a^2 - 4ab + 4b^2 - 1 = (a^2 - b^2)^2.\]Expanding, we get $a^4 - 2a^3 b - 3a^2 b^2 + 2ab^3 + b^4 - 1 = 0,$ or
\[(a^2 - ab + b^2)^2 - 3a^2 b^2 - 1 = 0,\]so
\[a^2 - ab + b^2 = \pm \sqrt{3a^2 b^2 + 1}.\]Thus, $b^2 - ab + a^2 \ge \frac{1}{4}.$

Conversely, suppose $b^2 - ab + a^2 \ge \frac{1}{4}.$  Let $d = b - a.$  Then $d^2 + 3ab \ge \frac{1}{4},$ which leads to $4d^2 + 12ab \ge 1.$  Then $4a^2 + 4ab + 4b^2 \ge 1,$ so $2a^2 + 2ab + 2b^2 \ge ab + 1.$  Also, $x^2 + (2b - 2a) x + (a^2 - 2b + 1 - b^2)$ has discriminant
\[(2b - 2a)^2 - 4(a^2 - 2b + 1 - b^2) = 4(b^2 - ab + a^2 - \frac{1}{4}).\]Since $b^2 - ab + a^2 - \frac{1}{4} \ge 0,$ the discriminant is nonnegative, which implies the graphs of $y = f(x)$ and $y = g(x)$ intersect.

Hence, the set of points $(a,b)$ for which the graphs of $y = f(x)$ and $y = g(x)$ do not intersect is
\[b^2 - ab + a^2 < \frac{1}{4}.\]This region is a ellipse with center $\left( 0, 0 \right)$ and semi-major axis $\frac{1}{2}$ and semi-minor axis $\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2},$ so its area is $\pi \cdot \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} = \boxed{\frac{\pi \sqrt{3}}{4}}.$

[asy]

dot("$-\frac{1}{2}$", (-1/2,0), SE);
dot("$(0,0)$", O, NE);
dot("$\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$", (0,sqrt(3)/2), NE);

Rearranging logs, the original equation becomes $$\frac{8}{\log n \log m}(\log x)^2 - \left(\frac{7}{\log n}+\frac{6}{\log m}\right)\log x - 2013 = 0$$By Vieta's Theorem, the sum of the possible values of $\log x$ is
\[\frac{\frac{7}{\log n}+\frac{6}{\log m}}{\frac{8}{\log n \log m}} = \frac{7\log m + 6 \log n}{8} = \log \sqrt[8]{m^7n^6}.\]But the sum of the possible values of $\log x$ is the logarithm of the product of the possible values of $x$. Thus the product of the possible values of $x$ is equal to $\sqrt[8]{m^7n^6}$.

It remains to minimize the integer value of $\sqrt[8]{m^7n^6}$. Since $m, n>1$, we can check that $m = 2^2$ and $n = 2^3$ work. Thus the answer is $4+8 = \boxed{12}$.

$v = \log_n x$ and $u = \log_m x$ is a common technique used to solve logarithmic equations that involve multiple bases. Substituting these variables, we get that $8uv - 7v - 6u - 2013 = 0$. To minimize the product $uv$, we want to minimize $u$ and $v$; thus, we take $(8u-6)$ and $(v-\tfrac{7}{8})$ to be as close to each other as possible. Since $u$ and $v$ are positive, $(8u-6) < (v-\tfrac{7}{8}) \implies (8u-6)(v-\tfrac{7}{8}) < 0.$ Thus, we take $8u-6$ to be negative and $v-\tfrac{7}{8}$ to be positive. &= 2a_n.

In order for the quadratic $kx^2 - 3kx + 4k + 7 = 0$ to have real roots, its discriminant must be nonnegative.  This gives us the inquality
\[(-3k)^2 - 4(k)(4k + 7) \ge 0.\]This expands as $-7k^2 - 28k \ge 0.$  This is equivalent to $k^2 + 4k \le 0,$ which factors as $k(k + 4) \le 0.$  The solution to this inequality is $-4 \le k \le 0.$  However, if $k = 0,$ then the given equation is not quadratic, so the set of $k$ which works is $\boxed{[-4,0)}.$

GPT File: datasets/persona_short_answer/persona_short_answer/intermediate_algebra/1886_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
The quadratic $kx^2 - 3kx + 4k + 7 = 0$ has real roots if and only if the discriminant is nonnegative. The discriminant is $$(-3k)^2 - 4(k)(4k+7) = 9k^2 - 16k^2 - 28k = -7k(2k+4).$$Therefore, the quadratic has real roots when $k \leq 0$ or $k \geq -2.$

\[\frac{|x - 13|}{(x - 13)(x + 4)} \le 0.\]Note that $x \neq -4.$  Also, the inequality is equivalent to $\frac{|x - 13|}{x - 13} \cdot \frac{1}{x + 4} \le 0.$  The critical points of the inequality are $-4$ and 13, so we can create the following table:

\begin{tabular}{c|ccc|c} 
& $x + 4$ & $x - 13$ & $\frac{|x - 13|}{x - 13}$ & $\frac{|x - 13|}{x - 13} \cdot \frac{1}{x + 4}$ \\ \hline

\end{tabular}Hence, the solution is

Note that if $r$ is a root, then so is $-r,$ so the roots are of the form $p,$ $-p,$ $q,$ $-q,$ for some complex numbers $p$ and $q.$  Since there are only two distinct roots, at least two of these values must be equal.

If $p = -p,$ then $p = 0$ is a root.  Hence, setting $x = 0,$ we must get 0.  In other words, $a^3 = 0,$ so $a = 0.$  But then the polynomial is
\[x^4 - x^2 = x^2 (x - 1)(x + 1) = 0,\]so there are three roots.  Hence, there are no solutions in this case.

Otherwise, $p = \pm q,$ so the roots are of the form $p,$ $p,$ $-p,$ $-p,$ and the quartic is
\[(x - p)^2 (x + p)^2 = x^4 - 2p^2 x^2 + p^4.\]Matching coefficients, we get $-2p^2 = a^2 - 1$ and $p^4 = a^3.$  Then $p^2 = \frac{1 - a^2}{2},$ so
\[\left( \frac{1 - a^2}{2} \right)^2 = a^3.\]This simplifies to $a^4 - 4a^3 - 2a^2 + 1 = 0.$

Let $f(x) = x^4 - 4x^3 - 2x^2 + 1.$  Since $f(0.51) > 0$ and $f(0.52) < 0,$ there is one root in the interval $(0.51,0.52).$  Since $f(4.43) < 0$ and $f(4.44) > 0,$ there is another root in the interval $(4.43,4.44).$  Factoring out these roots, we are left with a quadratic whose coefficients are approximately
\[x^2 + 0.95x + 0.44 = 0.\]The discriminant is negative, so this quadratic has two distinct, nonreal complex roots.  Therefore, all the roots of $a^4 - 4a^3 - 2a^2 + 1 = 0$ are distinct, and by Vieta's formulas, their sum is $\boxed{4}.$

Let $z$ be a complex root of $x^4 + (a^2 - 1) x^2 + a^3.$  Then
\begin{align*}
0 &= z^4 + (a^2 - 1) z^2 + a^3 \\
&= (z^2 + a) (z^2 - a + a^2 - 1).
\end{align*}Note that $z^2 + a = 0$ implies $z^2 = -a,$ so
\[x^4 + (a^2 - 1) x^2 + a^3 = (x^2 - \sqrt{a} i)(x^2 + \sqrt{a} i)(x^2 + \sqrt{a^2 - a + 1})(x^2 - \sqrt{a^2 - a + 1}).\]Thus, for two distinct roots, we need $a$ such that $a^2 - a + 1 < a,$ or
\[a^2 - 2a + 1 = (a - 1)^2 < 0.\]This is not possible, so there are no such values of $a.$

Hence, the sum of all complex values of $a$ is $\boxed{0}.$

&=F_{3n-1} + F_{3n-2} \\

We can start by factoring the polynomials in the numerator and denominator, which gives us
$$\frac{x(2x-3)}{x(x-1)} + 5x -11 = \frac{(x+1)(3x+2)}{(x+1)(x-1)}.$$If $x \ne 0$ and $x \ne -1$, we can cancel out some factors to get
$$\frac{2x-3}{x-1} + 5x -11 = \frac{3x+2}{x-1}.$$Moving the fractional terms to one side gives us
$$ 5x -11 = \frac{x+5}{x-1}.$$Now we can eliminate the denominator by multiplying by $x-1$ on both sides (as long as $x\ne1$) and then move all the terms to one side,
$$5x^2- 17x + 6 = 0.$$Factoring gives us

$2x^2-3x = (2x-x)(x-0) = 2x^2 -x^2-3x = -x^2-3x$ so the left-hand side simplifies as follows. \begin{align*}
\frac{2x^2-3x}{x^2-x} + 5x -11 &= \frac{-x^2-3x}{x(x-1)} + 5x -11\\
&= \frac{-x-3}{x-1} + 5x -11\\
&= \frac{-x-3 + 5x^2 - 11x(x-1)}{x(x-1)}\\
&= \frac{5x^2 -12x - 3}{x(x-1)}
\end{align*}The right-hand side simplifies as follows. \begin{align*}
\frac{3x^2+5x+2}{x^2-1} &= \frac{(3x+2)(x+1)}{(x+1)(x-1)}\\
&= \frac{3x+2}{x-1}.
\end{align*}Thus, we want to solve the equation $$\frac{5x^2 -12x - 3}{x(x-1)} = \frac{3x+2}{x-1}.$$If $x=0,$ then the left-hand side is undefined and the right-hand side is equal to $2,$ so $x=0$ is not a solution. If $x=1,$ then both sides of the equation are undefined since we would be dividing by zero.

If $x \neq 0, 1,$ then we can rearrange the equation as follows. $$\frac{5x^2 -12x - 3}{x(x-1)} = \frac{3x+2}{x-1} \quad \Rightarrow \quad (5x^2 -12x - 3)(x-1) = (3x+2)x(x-1).$$This simplifies to the quadratic equation $5x^2-4x-6=0,$ which factors as $(5x+6)(x-1) = 0.$ Thus, the solutions are $x=-\frac{6}{5}$ and $x=1,$ but we must exclude $x=1$ from the solution set since this makes both sides of the equation undefined. Hence, the solution is $\boxed{-\frac{6}{5}}.$

Let $x = \log_a b,$ $y = \log_b c,$ and $z = \log_c a.$  Then $a^x = b,$ $b^y = c,$ and $c^z = a,$ so
\begin{align*}
\log_a c &= \log_a b + \log_b c \\
&= x + y \\
\log_a a &= 1 \\
&= z \cdot \log_c a \\
1 &= z \frac{1}{z} \\
&= z^2 \cdot \frac{1}{z} \\
&= z^2 \log_c a \\
1 &= z^3.
\end{align*}We want to compute $x^3 + y^3 + z^3.  $From the identity
\[(x + y + z)((x + y + z)^2 - 3(xy + xz + yz))) = x^3 + y^3 + z^3 + 3(x + y)(y + z)(z + x),\]we see that
\begin{align*}
x^3 + y^3 + z^3 &= (x + y + z)((x + y + z)^2 - 3(xy + xz + yz)) - 3(x + y)(y + z)(z + x) \\

Extracting Answer from: 
First, we claim that any quartic with real coefficients can be written as the product of two quadratic polynomials with real coefficients.

Let $z$ be a complex root of the quartic.  If $z$ is not real, then its complex conjugate $\overline{z}$ is also a root.  Then the quadratic $(x - z)(x - \overline{z})$ has real coefficients, and when we factor out this quadratic, we are left with a quadratic that also has real coefficients.

If $z$ is real, then we can factor out $x - z,$ leaving us with a cubic with real coefficients.  Every cubic with real coefficients has at least one real roots, say $w.$  Then we can factor out $x - w,$ leaving us with a quadratic with real coefficients.  The product of this quadratic and $(x - z)(x - w)$ is the original quartic.

So, let
\[x^4 + ax^3 + 3x^2 + bx + 1 = (x^2 + px + r) \left( x^2 + qx + \frac{1}{r} \right), \quad (*)\]where $p,$ $q,$ and $r$ are real.

Suppose one quadratic factor has distinct real roots, say $z$ and $w.$  Then the only way that the quartic can be nonnegative for all real numbers $x$ is if the roots of the other quadratic are also $z$ and $w.$  Thus, we can write the quadratic as
\[(x - z)^2 (x - w)^2.\]Thus, we can assume that for each quadratic factor, the quadratic does not have real, distinct roots.  This implies that the discriminant of each quadratic is at most 0.  Thus,
\[p^2 \le 4r \quad \text{and} \quad q^2 \le \frac{4}{r}.\]It follows that $r > 0.$  Multiplying these inequalities, we get
\[p^2 q^2 \le 16,\]so $|pq| \le 4.$

Expanding $(*)$ and matching coefficients, we get
\begin{align*}
p + q &= a, \\
pq + r + \frac{1}{r} &= 3, \\
\frac{p}{r} + qr &= b.
\end{align*}Therefore,
\begin{align*}
a^2 + b^2 &= (p + q)^2 + \left( \frac{p}{r} + qr \right)^2 \\
&= p^2 + 2pq + q^2 + \frac{p^2}{r^2} + 2pq + q^2 r^2 \\
&= p^2 + 4pq + q^2 + \frac{p^2}{r^2} + q^2 r^2 \\
&\le 4r + 4pq + \frac{4}{r} + \frac{4r}{r^2} + \frac{4}{r} \cdot r^2 \\
&= 4pq + 8r + \frac{8}{r}.
\end{align*}From the equation $pq + r + \frac{1}{r} = 3,$
\[r + \frac{1}{r} = 3 - pq,\]so
\[a^2 + b^2 \le 4pq + 8(3 - pq) = 24 - 4pq \le 40.\]To obtain equality, we must have $pq = -4$ and $r + \frac{1}{r} = 7.$  This leads to $r^2 - 7r + 1 = 0,$ whose roots are real and positive.  For either root $r,$ we can set $p = \sqrt{4r}$ and $q = -\sqrt{\frac{4}{r}},$ which shows that equality is possible.  For example, we can obtain the quartic
\[\left( x - \frac{3 + \sqrt{5}}{2} \right)^2 \left( x + \frac{3 - \sqrt{5}}{2} \right)^2 = x^4 - 2x^3 \sqrt{5} + 3x^2 + 2x \sqrt{5} + 1.\]Hence, the maximum value of $a^2 + b^2$ is $\boxed{40}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Note that
\begin{align*}
x^4 + ax^3 + 3x^2 + bx + 1 &= \left( x^2 + \frac{a}{2} x \right)^2 - \left( \frac{a^2}{4} x^2 - 3x^2 - bx - 1 \right) \\
&= \left( x^2 + \frac{a}{2} x \right)^2 - \left( \frac{a^2}{4} x^2 + bx + \frac{13}{4} \right).
\end{align*}We want this expression to be nonnegative for all $x.$

Let $y = \frac{a}{2} x.$  Then the inequality becomes
\[y^2 - \left( \frac{a^2}{4} y^2 + by + \frac{13}{4} \right) \ge 0\]for all $y.$  In turn, this means
\[\left( \frac{3}{4} a^2 - 1 \right) y^2 - by - \frac{13}{4} \ge 0\]for all $y.$

For this quadratic to be always nonnegative, the discriminant of this quadratic is nonpositive.  Hence,

If $a_n =an + b$ and $b_n = cn + d$ are two arithmetic sequences, then their term-wise product takes the form \[a_nb_n = (an+b)(cn+d) = An^2 + Bn + C,\]where $A, B, C$ are constants. Therefore, some sequence $x_n = An^2 + Bn + C$ has $x_0 = 1440,$ $x_1 = 1716,$ and $x_2 = 1848.$ Then we have the equations \[\begin{aligned} C &= 1440, \\ A+B+C&=1716,\\  4A+2B+C&=1848. \end{aligned}\]Subtracting $C=1440$ from the second and third equations, we have $A+B=276$ and $4A+2B=408,$ or $2A+B=204.$ Then \[A = (2A+B) - (A+B) = 204 - 276 = -72,\]and so $B = 276-A=348.$ We conclude that the given sequence has the general formula \[x_n = -72n^2 + 348n + 1440.\]Then the eighth term is \[\begin{aligned} x_7 &= -72 \cdot 7^2 + 348 \cdot 7 + 1440 = \boxed{348}. \end{aligned}\]

\[\frac{5x + (1 - x) + (1 - x) + (1 - x) + (1 - x) + (1 - x)}{6} \ge \sqrt[6]{5x (1 - x)^5}.\]This simplifies to $\sqrt[6]{5x (1 - x)^5} \le \frac{5}{6}.$  (Note how the left hand-side simplifies to a constant, which is why we consider $5x(1 - x)^5.$)  Hence,

Because the problem asks for the sum of the lengths of the intervals, we can replace $x$ with $x-2010,$ and the answer will not change. Then we have the inequality $$\frac{1}{x+1}+\frac{1}{x}+\frac{1}{x-1}\ge1.$$Let $f(x)=\frac{1}{x+1}+\frac{1}{x}+\frac{1}{x-1}$. Note that $f(x)$ has three vertical asymptotes at $x=-1, 0, 1.$ Since the function $g(x) = 1/x$ is decreasing over every connected interval on which it is defined, the same is true of $f(x).$ That is, $f(x)$ is decreasing on each of the intervals $(-\infty, -1),$ $(-1, 0),$ $(0,1),$ and $(1, \infty).$

As $x$ approaches $\infty$ and as $x$ approaches $-\infty,$ we see that $f(x)$ approaches $0.$ And as $x$ approaches each of the vertical asymptotes $x=-1, 0, 1$ from the left, $f(x)$ approaches $-\infty,$ while as $x$ approaches each of the vertical asymptotes from the right, $f(x)$ approaches $\infty.$ This lets us sketch the graph of $f(x),$ as shown below:
[asy]
size(12cm);
void axes(real x0, real x1, real y0, real y1)
{
    draw((x0,0)--(x1,0),EndArrow);
    draw((0,y0)--(0,y1),EndArrow);
    label("$x$",(x1,0),E);
    label("$y$",(0,y1),N);
    for (int i=floor(x0)+1; i<x1; ++i)
        draw((i,.1)--(i,-.1));
    for (int i=floor(y0)+1; i<y1; ++i)
        draw((.1,i)--(-.1,i));
}
axes(-5.9,7,-4.6,5);
real f(real x) { return 1/(x+1) + 1/x + 1/(x-1); }
draw(graph(f,-5.5,-1.4),Arrows);
draw((-1,-4.5)--(-1,4.5),dotted);
draw(graph(f,-.84,-.23),Arrows);
draw(graph(f,.2,.82),Arrows);
draw((1,-4.5)--(1,4.5),dotted);
draw(graph(f,1.3,6.5),Arrows);
draw((-2,1)--(5,1),blue,Arrows);
dot((-.675,1)^^(.4608,1)^^(3.214,1),blue);
[/asy]
Therefore, the equation $f(x) = 1$ has three roots $p, q, r,$ where $p \in (-1, 0),$ $q \in (0, 1),$ and $r \in (1, \infty).$ In terms of these roots, the values of $x$ such that $f(x) \ge 1$ are \[(-1, p] \cup (0, q] \cup (1, r].\]The sum of the lengths of the three intervals above is $(p+1) + q + (r-1) = p+q+r,$ so we want to find the sum of the roots of $f(x) = 1.$

Multiplying the equation $f(x) = \frac1{x+1} + \frac1x + \frac1{x-1} = 1$ by $(x+1)x(x-1)$ and then rearranging, we get the cubic equation \[x^3 - 3x^2 - x + 1 = 0.\]By Vieta's formulas, the sum of the roots of this equation is $\boxed{3}.$

By the Integer Root Theorem, any integer root must be a divisor of the constant term -- thus, in this case, a (positive or negative) divisor of $72$. However, this leaves quite a lot of candidates:
$$\pm 1,\ \pm 2,\ \pm 3,\ \pm 4,\ \pm 6,\ \pm 8,\ \pm 9,\ \pm 12,\ \pm 18,\ \pm 24,\ \pm 36,\ \pm 72.$$To narrow our choices, we define another polynomial.  Note that $g(1) = 77.$  Then by the Factor Theorem, $g(x) - 77$ is divisible by $x - 1.$  In other words,
$$g(x) = (x-1)q(x) + 77$$for some polynomial $q(x)$. Thus if we define $h(x) = g(x+1)$, then we have
$$h(x) = xq(x+1) + 77,$$so $h(x)$ has a constant term of $77$. Thus any integer root of $h(x)$ is a divisor of $77$; the possibilities are
$$-77,\ -11,\ -7,\ -1,\ 1,\ 7,\ 11,\ 77.$$This is useful because, if $x$ is a root of $g(x)$, then $h(x-1)=g(x)=0$, so $x-1$ must appear in the list of roots of $h(x)$. In particular, $x$ must be $1$ more than a root of $h(x)$, which gives the possibilities
$$-76,\ -10,\ -6,\ 0,\ 2,\ 8,\ 12,\ 78.$$Of these, only $-6$, $2$, $8$, and $12$ were candidates in our original list. Testing them one by one, we find that $x=\boxed{12}$ is the only integer root of $g(x)$.

We can write the function as
\[f(x) = \sqrt{(7 - x)(3 + x)} - \sqrt{(5 - x)(2 + x)}.\]This shows that the function is defined only for $-2 \le x \le 5.$  Also, $(7 - x)(3 + x) - (5 - x)(2 + x) = x + 11 > 0$ on this interval, which means that $f(x)$ is always positive.

Then
\begin{align*}
[f(x)]^2 &= (7 - x)(3 + x) - 2 \sqrt{(7 - x)(3 + x)} \sqrt{(5 - x)(2 + x)} + (5 - x)(2 + x) \\
&= -2x^2 + 7x + 31 - 2 \sqrt{(7 - x)(2 + x)(5 - x)(3 + x)} \\
&= 2 + (7 - x)(2 + x) - 2 \sqrt{(7 - x)(2 + x)} \sqrt{(5 - x)(3 + x)} + (5 - x)(3 + x) \\
&= 2 + \left[ \sqrt{(7 - x)(2 + x)} - \sqrt{(5 - x)(3 + x)} \right]^2 \ge 2.
\end{align*}Therefore, $f(x) \ge \sqrt{2}.$

Equality occurs when $(7 - x)(2 + x) = (5 - x)(3 + x),$ or $x = \frac{1}{3}.$  We conclude that the minimum value is $\boxed{\sqrt{2}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
We factor the expressions under the radicals to get
\[f(x) = \sqrt{-(x - 2)(x + 7)} - \sqrt{-(x - 1)(x + 4)}.\]We assume $x$ is real, so $-(x - 2)(x + 7) \ge 0$ and $-(x - 1)(x + 4) \ge 0,$ which means $-7 \le x \le -2$ or $1 \le x \le 4.$  Let $a = \sqrt{-(x - 2)(x + 7)},$ and let $b = \sqrt{-(x - 1)(x + 4)}.$  Then $f(x) = a - b.$

Since both $-7 \le x \le -2$ and $1 \le x \le 4$ satisfy $0 \le (x - 2)(x + 7)$ and $0 \le (x - 1)(x + 4),$ we can assume that $a$ and $b$ are positive.

By Cauchy-Schwarz,
\[(a - b)(1 + 1) \ge (a - b)^2 = a^2 - 2ab + b^2,\]so $2(a - b) \ge a^2 - 2ab + b^2.$  We can write this as
\[(a - b)^2 + 2(a - b)(a + b - 2b) + 4b^2 \ge 0,\]or
\[(a - b + a + b - 2b)^2 + 4b^2 \ge 0.\]Hence, $(2a - 3b)^2 + 5b^2 \ge 0,$ which means $2a - 3b = 0.$  Hence, $a = \frac{3}{2} b,$ and
\[f(x) = \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{-(x - 2)(x + 7)} - \sqrt{-(x - 1)(x + 4)}.\]By AM-GM, $\sqrt{-(x - 1)(x + 4)} \le \frac{3}{2},$ so
\[-2 \le f(x) \le \frac{1}{2}.\]The minimum value is $\boxed{-2},$ which occurs for $x = -2.$

\[
\begin{array}{ccccccccccc}

\end{array}

The inequality $\frac{ab + 1}{a + b} < \frac{3}{2}$ turn into
\[ab + 1 < \frac{3}{2} a + \frac{3}{2} b.\]Then
\[ab - \frac{3}{2} a - \frac{3}{2} b + 1 < 0.\]Applying Simon's Favorite Factoring Trick, we get
\[\left( a - \frac{3}{2} \right) \left( b - \frac{3}{2} \right) < \frac{5}{4}.\]Hence,
\[(2a - 3)(2b - 3) < 5.\]If $a = 1,$ then the inequality becomes
\[3 - 2b < 5,\]which is satisfied for any positive integer $b.$  Similarly, if $b = 1,$ then the inequality is satisfied for any positive integer $a.$

Otherwise, $a \ge 2$ and $b \ge 2,$ so $2a - 3 \ge 1$ and $2b - 3 \ge 1.$  Note that both $2a - 3$ and $2b - 3$ are odd, so $(2a - 3)(2b - 3)$ is odd, so their product can only be 1 or 3.  This leads us to the solutions $(a,b) = (2,2),$ $(2,3),$ and $(3,2).$

If $a = 1,$ then
\[\frac{a^3 b^3 + 1}{a^3 + b^3} = \frac{b^3 + 1}{1 + b^3} = 1.\]Similarly, if $b = 1,$ then the expression also simplifies to 1.

For $(a,b) = (2,2),$
\[\frac{a^3 b^3 + 1}{a^3 + b^3} = \frac{2^3 \cdot 2^3 + 1}{2^3 + 2^3} = \frac{65}{16}.\]For $(a,b) = (2,3)$ or $(3,2),$
\[\frac{a^3 b^3 + 1}{a^3 + b^3} = \frac{2^3 \cdot 3^3 + 1}{2^3 + 3^3} = \frac{31}{5}.\]Hence, the largest possible value of the expression is $\boxed{\frac{31}{5}}.$

Let $x=a+b.$ Note that $\frac{ab+1}{x}<\frac32$ is equivalent to $2ab+2<x\cdot 3=3x.$ Adding $5a^2b^2$ to both sides gives $a^2b^2+(ab+1)^2<3x^2,$ or
\begin{align*} a^2b^2+(ab+1)^2(=a^2b^2+(x-1)^2)<3x^2. \end{align*}
Let $k=\frac{(x-1)^2}{a^2}.$ Then $\frac{(x-1)^2}{b^2}=3x^2-\frac{(ab+1)^2}{b^2}-a^2\overset{\mathrm{AM-GM}}{\geq}3x^2-\frac43a^2-\frac43b^2,$ so $\frac{(x-1)^2}{b^2}>3x^2-\frac43a^2-\frac43b^2,$ or $a^2k+b^2>3(a^2+b^2)k+2(x-1).$ Rearranging gives $(3a^2-ak-3b^2)+(3b^2-bk-3a^2)<6x-4,$ or
\begin{align*} (3a-b)(b-3a+2k)+(3b-a)(a-3b+2k)<6x-4. \end{align*}
Therefore $(3a-b)(b-3a+2k)+(3b-a)(a-3b+2k)\leq 6x-5.$ Since the maximum of $\frac{a^3b^3+1}{a^3+b^3}$ occurs when $a^2k+b^2$ is maximized, we wish to maximize $k.$ By the Trivial Inequality and AM-GM,
\begin{align*} 0\leq 3(a-b)^2 &\leq (a+b)^2\\ &=x^2\\ &=a^2+b^2+2kab+2x-2a-2b\\ &\leq a^2+b^2+2kab+2x-2\sqrt{(a^2+b^2)(1+k)}\\ &\leq a^2+b^2+2kab+2x-2\sqrt{\left(\sqrt{3(a^2b^2+(a+b)^2)}\right)^2(1+k)}\\ &=a^2+b^2+2ab\sqrt{3(1+k)}+2x-2\sqrt{3(a^2b^2+(a+b)^2)(1+k)}\\ &\leq a^2+b^2+2ab\sqrt{3(1+k)}+2x-\sqrt{12(x^2-2)+12(x^2-1)k+4}\\ &\leq a^2+b^2+2ab\sqrt{3(1+k)}+2x-2\sqrt{6x^2-8}. \end{align*}
Subtracting $2a^2b^2$ from both sides gives $3a^2(b-3a+2k)+3b^2(a-3b+2k)+6ab\sqrt{3(1+k)}\leq 6x-\sqrt{24x^2-32}-2.$ Since this inequality holds for all $a$ and $b,$ its left-hand side is nonpositive, so we have \begin{align*} 6x-\sqrt{24x^2-32}-2&<0\\ x&<\frac{\sqrt{6}+\sqrt{10}}{4}. \end{align*} Therefore the answer is $\boxed{\frac{677}{1000}}$.

Minimizing $\frac{x + y}{x - y}$ is equivalent to minimizing
\[\frac{x + y}{x - y} + 1 = \frac{2x}{x - y} = \frac{-2x}{y - x}.\]Note that $-2x$ and $y - x$ are always positive, so to minimize this expression, we take $y = 4,$ the largest possible value of $y.$

Then minimizing $\frac{x + 4}{x - 4}$ is equivalent to minimizing
\[\frac{x + 4}{x - 4} - 1 = \frac{8}{x - 4} = -\frac{8}{4 - x}.\]Note that $4 - x$ is always positive, so to minimize this expression, we take $x = -3.$  Hence, the minimum value is $\frac{-3 + 4}{-3 - 4} = \boxed{-\frac{1}{7}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Let $r$ and $s$ be the roots of $f(x),$ so that $r = 1 + 2\sqrt{3}$ and $s = 3 - \sqrt{2}.$ Then $r'$ and $s'$ are also roots, where $r' = 1 - 2\sqrt{3}$ and $s' = 3 + \sqrt{2}.$ By Vieta's formulas, we have
\begin{align*}
r+s+r'+s' &= -\frac{a_3}{a_4} = -\frac{0}{a_4} = 0 \\
rs+rs'+r's+r's' &= \frac{a_2}{a_4} = \frac{\text{sum of roots taken two at a time}}{a_4}.
\end{align*}
We have that
\begin{align*}
r+s &= 1+2\sqrt 3+3-\sqrt 2 = 4 + 2\sqrt{3}-\sqrt{2} \\
r's' &= (1+2\sqrt{3})(3+\sqrt{2}) = 7\sqrt{3}+\sqrt{54} = 7\sqrt{3}+3\sqrt{6},
\end{align*}
so
\[rs+rs'+r's+r's' = (4+2\sqrt{3}-\sqrt{2})^2 - rs = 20 + 16 \sqrt{3}.\]
Then
\[f(x) = a_4(x-r)(x-s)(x-r')(x-s') = a_4 \bigl((x-r)(x-s) + (x-r')(x-s')\bigr)(x-c) = a_4\bigl(x^3 - tx^2 + ux - v\bigr),\]
where $c$ is the final root, $a_4$ is the leading coefficient, and $t$, $u$, and $v$ are the coefficients that we need to find. We see that $t = r+s+r'+s' = 0$ and $u = rs+rs'+r's+r's' = 20 + 16 \sqrt{3},$ so
\[f(x) = a_4(x^3 + ux - v).\]
Finally,
\begin{align*}
-f(0)&= a_4(0^3+u\cdot0-v) = -a_4 v = 154 \\
\implies a_4&= \frac{-154}{v} \\
\implies f(1) &= a_4(1^3 + u\cdot 1 - v) = \frac{-154}{v}(1+u-v).
\end{align*}
It remains to determine $v.$ Note that by Vieta's formulas, the sum of the roots of $f(x)$ is  $(r+s+r'+s')+c.$ We have that $-t = r+s+r'+s' = 0$ and $u = rs+rs'+r's+r's' = 20 + 16 \sqrt{3},$ so
\[(r+s+r'+s')+c = r+s+r'+s'-\frac{-v}{u} = (4+2\sqrt{3}-\sqrt{2}) + (3-\sqrt{2}) - \frac{v}{20+16\sqrt{3}} = 7 \implies c = -4-2\sqrt{3}.\]
Now we have that
\begin{align*}
(r+s)(r'+s') + (r-r')(s-s') &= (1+2\sqrt{3}+3-\sqrt{2})\cdot(1-2\sqrt{3}+3+\sqrt{2}) + (1+2\sqrt{3}-1+2\sqrt{3})(3-\sqrt{2}-3+\sqrt{2}) \\
&= (4+2\sqrt{3}-\sqrt{2})(4-2\sqrt{3}-\sqrt{2}) + (4\sqrt{3})^2 \\
&= 28.
\end{align*}
Thus, $f(x) = a_4(x^3 + 20x - 28-16\sqrt{3})$ and $a_4 = \frac{154}{28+16\sqrt{3}} = \frac{77-44\sqrt{3}}{19}.$ It follows that
\begin{align*}
f(1) &= a_4 (1^3 + 20\cdot 1 - 28-16\sqrt{3}) \\
&= \frac{77-44\sqrt{3}}{19}\cdot (-7-16\sqrt{3}).
\end{align*}Simplifying, we get $\boxed{-289-492\sqrt{3}}.$

By Cauchy-Schwarz,
\[(3^2 + 4^2)(b^2 + c^2) \ge (3b + 4c)^2.\]Since $a + 3b + 4c = a^2 + b^2 + c^2 = 25,$ we can write this as
\[25(25 - a^2) \ge (25 - a)^2.\]Expanding, we get
\[625 - 25a^2 \ge 625 - 50a + a^2,\]so $26a^2 - 50a \le 0.$  This factors as $2a(13a - 25) \le 0.$  This implies $a \le \frac{25}{13}.$

We have that
\begin{align*}
(a + 3b + 4c)^2 &= a^2 + 9b^2 + 16c^2 + 6ab + 8ac + 24bc \\
&= (a^2 + b^2 + c^2) + 8b^2 + 15c^2 + 2ab + 2ac + 24bc \\
&= 25 + 8b^2 + 15c^2 + 2a(b + c) + 24bc \\
&\le 25 + 8b^2 + 15c^2 + 2 \sqrt{(b^2 + c^2)(a^2 + (b + c)^2)} + 24bc \\
&= 25 + 8b^2 + 15c^2 + 2 \sqrt{(25 - b^2 - c^2)(a^2 + (b + c)^2)} + 24bc.
\end{align*}Thus,
\[a^2 - (a + 3b + 4c)^2 + 25 \ge 2 \sqrt{(25 - b^2 - c^2)(a^2 + (b + c)^2)}.\]Squaring both sides, we get
\[(a + 2b + 2c)(3a - 2b - 2c) (11a - 26b - 33c) \ge 0.\]Since $a = 25 - 3b - 4c,$ this becomes
\[-2b^2 + 13bc + 12c^2 \le 0,\]which factors as $2(c - b)(6c + b) \ge 0.$  Then $c \ge b.$

If $b = c,$ then $2b^2 + a = 25,$ so $a = 25 - 2b^2.$  We want to maximize $a,$ so this occurs when $b$ is largest, which means $b = c = \frac{5}{\sqrt{2}},$ so $a = \frac{15}{\sqrt{2}},$ and the maximum is $\boxed{\frac{15}{\sqrt{2}}}.$

Let $z = x + yi,$ where $x$ and $y$ are real numbers.

From the equation $\left| \frac{z - 4}{z - 8} \right| = 1,$ $|z - 4| = |z - 8|.$  Then
\[|x + yi - 4| = |x + yi - 8|,\]so $(x - 4)^2 + y^2 = (x - 8)^2 + y^2.$  This simplifies to $x = 6.$

From the equation $\left| \frac{z - 12}{z - 8i} \right| = \frac{5}{3},$ $3|z - 12| = 5|z - 8i|.$  Then
\[3|6 + yi - 12| = 5|6 + yi - 8i|,\]so $9(36 + y^2) = 25(36 + (y - 8)^2).$  This simplifies to $16y^2 - 400y + 2176 = 0,$ which factors as $16(y - 8)(y - 17) = 0.$  Hence, $y = 8$ or $y = 17.$

Thus, the solutions in $z$ are $\boxed{6 + 8i, 6 + 17i}.$

\[|z - 12| = \frac{5}{3} |z - 8i|.\]Let $z = x + yi.$  Then the first equation becomes
\begin{align*}
(x - 4)^2 + y^2 &= (x - 8)^2 + y^2 \\
2x &= 6,
\end{align*}so $x = 3.$  Then $|z - 4| = |z - 8| = 5.$

The second equation becomes
\begin{align*}
(x - 12)^2 + y^2 &= \frac{25}{9} [(x - 8)^2 + y^2] \\

\frac{16}{9} \cdot 9 + y^2 &= 16 \cdot 3 + 16y + \frac{256}{9} \\
y &= \pm \sqrt{\frac{16}{9} - \frac{256}{9 \cdot 9}} \\
&= \pm \frac{2 \sqrt{5}}{3}.
\end{align*}Thus, the solutions are $\boxed{3 + \frac{2 \sqrt{5}}{3}i, 3 - \frac{2 \sqrt{5}}{3}i}.$

\[\frac{\lfloor \sqrt[3]{n}\rfloor^3}{\lfloor \sqrt[3]{n} \rfloor} \leq n \leq \frac{(\lfloor \sqrt[3]{n}\rfloor + 1)^3 - 1}{\lfloor \sqrt[3]{n} \rfloor}\]
So if $\lfloor \sqrt[3]{n}\rfloor =k$, we know $n$ must be in the range $k^3 \leq n < (k+1)^3$.

Say $d$ is the number of divisors of $k$. Then we can find $d$ integers $a_1, a_2, \cdots a_d$ such that $k=a_1precalc
Note that $\lfloor\frac{n_p}{k}\rfloor$ is the number of possible $n_i$'s, such that $\lfloor\sqrt[3]{n_i}\rfloor = k$. Now, note that there are $\frac{(k+1)^3-k^3}{k+1-k}$ ways to pick an $n_i$ between $k^3$ and $(k+1)^3$, so $\lfloor\frac{n_p}{k}\rfloor = (k+1)^2 - k^2 = 2k+1$. Therefore, since $\frac{n_p}{k}$ is an integer, $n_p = k(2k+1)$. Great. Now, note that $\lfloor\frac{n_i}{k}\rfloor$ for all $n_i$ is at most $2k$, since every integer from $1$ to $(k+1)^3-k^3$ is included in the range $k\lfloor\frac{n_i}{k}\rfloor$ for some $n_i$, and for $k<\sqrt{3}$ this is at most $2k$ numbers. Hence, we have the following inequalities \[\frac{n_1}{k} \leq \frac{n_p}{k} \leq (2k+1)k\] \[\Rightarrow \frac{n_1}{k} \leq (2k+1) \leq 7 \cdot \frac{n_1}{k} \cdots (\ast)\].
Since the ratios are the same for each $n_1, n_2, ..., n_{70}$, we will maximize $\frac{n_1}{k}$ to obtain our answer. Note that $(\ast)$ shows that $2k+1$ must be an integer divisor of $\frac{n_1}{k}$, hence $2k+1$ must be less than or equal to $\sqrt{\frac{n_1}{k}}$, since it is a divisor. Therefore, $2k+1 \leq \sqrt{(k+1)^3-k^3} \leq 3.3$. Trying $2k+1=7$ shows that it works, hence the maximal possible value of $\frac{n_i}{k}$ is $7^\frac{3}{2}$, so our answer is $7^2 = \boxed{49}$.
